}

             <div class="main section" id="main" name="Main">
              <div class="widget Blog" id="Blog1">
               <div class="blog-posts hfeed">
                <div class="date-outer">
                 <div class="date-posts">
                  <div class="post-outer">
                   <div class="post hentry uncustomized-post-template" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/BlogPosting">
                    <meta content="https://lh3.ggpht.com/-OPMKc8CxRc4/UZVBk5axbxI/AAAAAAAAAa0/DFDHMmeH_dA/s72-c/IMG_1845CROP.jpg" itemprop="image_url"/>
                    <meta content="7801024861045889357" itemprop="blogId"/>
                    <meta content="5760005830767387300" itemprop="postId"/>
                    <a name="5760005830767387300">
                    </a>
                    <h3 class="post-title entry-title" itemprop="name">
                     FREE INFINITY SCARF PATTERN FOR BEGINNERS
                    </h3>
                    <div class="post-header">
                     <div class="post-header-line-1">
                     </div>
                    </div>
                    <div class="post-body entry-content" id="post-body-5760005830767387300" itemprop="articleBody">
                     As promised, here is my
                     <b>
                      free infinity scarf pattern
                     </b>
                     for
                     <b>
                      beginners
                     </b>
                     ! You too can knit something
                     <b>
                      trendy
                     </b>
                     and fun. I have been so excited to share this with you all and its finally here!
                     <br/>
                     <br/>
                     <div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;">
                      <a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-OPMKc8CxRc4/UZVBk5axbxI/AAAAAAAAAa0/DFDHMmeH_dA/s1600/IMG_1845CROP.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="">
                       <img alt="FREE INFINITY SCARF PATTERN" border="0" height="210" src="https://lh3.ggpht.com/-OPMKc8CxRc4/UZVBk5axbxI/AAAAAAAAAa0/DFDHMmeH_dA/s280/IMG_1845CROP.jpg" title="FREE INFINITY SCARF PATTERN" width="280"/>
                      </a>
                     </div>
                     <br/>
                     SEED STITCH INFINITY SCARF
                     <br/>
                     <br/>
                     Materials you will need:
                     <br/>
                     <ul>
                      <li>
                       <i>
                        3 hanks / 300g of
                        <a href="http://www.knitpicks.com/yarn-fiber/new-yarn-lines/billow.html">
                         Billow by KnitPicks
                        </a>
                       </i>
                      </li>
                      <li>
                       <i>
                        size 11 US straight needles that are at least 10 inches in length
                       </i>
                      </li>
                      <li>
                       <i>
                        scissors
                       </i>
                      </li>
                      <li>
                       <i>
                        darning needle
                       </i>
                      </li>
                     </ul>
                     Turn your hank into a ball by following my instructions
                     <a href="http://www.tinselmint.com/2013/05/how-to-turn-your-hank-of-yarn-into-ball.html">
                      here
                     </a>
                     . If you are an advanced knitter and would like to make a substitution this is a bulky weight super soft pima cotton yarn with a thick and thin texture. If you are not familiar with US needle sizes you can find my conversion chart
                     <a href="http://www.tinselmint.com/2013/04/size-matters-yarn-and-knitting-needles.html">
                      here
                     </a>
                     .
                     <i>
                     </i>
                     <i>
                     </i>
                     <br/>
                     <i>
                      <br/>
                     </i>
                     Gauge:
                     <br/>
                     <br/>
                     6.5 stitches x 8 rows = 2 inches square
                     <br/>
                     <br/>
                     I know we haven't talked about gauge yet, so for the purpose of this pattern I only want you to use this as a rough guide. If the gauge of your knitted piece is slightly different that's ok because this is a very stretchy knit.
                     <br/>
                     <br/>
                     Pattern:
                     <br/>
                     <br/>
                     Cast on 33 stitches (click
                     <a href="http://www.tinselmint.com/2013/04/how-to-knit-casting-on-for-beginners.html">
                      here
                     </a>
                     for instructions on how to cast on).
                     <br/>
                     <br/>
                     <div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;">
                      <a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-Nt1aTybXiwg/UX3qjyhbjEI/AAAAAAAAAXI/TVbW1ZUKoig/s1600/IMG_1708crop.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="">
                       <img alt="CAST ON" border="0" height="210" src="https://lh3.ggpht.com/-Nt1aTybXiwg/UX3qjyhbjEI/AAAAAAAAAXI/TVbW1ZUKoig/s280/IMG_1708crop.jpg" title="CAST ON " width="280"/>
                      </a>
                     </div>
                     This scarf is knit in what's called a
                     <b>
                      seed stitch
                     </b>
                     pattern. This pattern is created by alternating one knit stitch (k1) and one purl stitch (p1). You can find my instructions on how to knit
                     <a href="http://www.tinselmint.com/2013/04/how-to-knit-knit-stitch-with-video.html">
                      here
                     </a>
                     , and how to purl
                     <a href="http://www.tinselmint.com/2013/04/how-to-knit-purl-stitch-with-video.html">
                      here
                     </a>
                     . You'll have to remember to bring your working yarn (the yarn you wrap around the needle) from the front of your work to the back of your work when alternating between knitting and purling.
                     <br/>
                     <br/>
                     <div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;">
                      <a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-RfOyrlz2CRU/UXgIpoSiE8I/AAAAAAAAAXI/QINGSYZCkvc/s1600/IMG_1729.JPG" imageanchor="1" style="">
                       <img alt="SEED STITCH" border="0" height="210" src="https://lh3.ggpht.com/-RfOyrlz2CRU/UXgIpoSiE8I/AAAAAAAAAXI/QINGSYZCkvc/s280/IMG_1729.JPG" title="SEED STITCH" width="280"/>
                      </a>
                     </div>
                     <br/>
                     First row: (k1, p1) repeat until one stitch remains on your needle, k1. Turn work over.
                     <br/>
                     <br/>
                     Repeat the first row until your scarf measures 62 inches in length or approximately 232 rows (width is 10.5 inches).
                     <br/>
                     <br/>
                     Cast off (click
                     <a href="http://www.tinselmint.com/2013/04/how-to-knit-casting-off-for-beginners.html" target="_blank">
                      here
                     </a>
                     for instructions on how to cast off). Use scissors to break yarn, leaving a long tail of approximately 22 inches for stitching together the two ends of your scarf.
                     <br/>
                     <br/>
                     To complete your infinity scarf, lay it on a flat surface and bring the two ends together (while still lying flat) so that they meet. Stitch the two ends together using the tail from your cast off edge. Next darn in any ends using a darning needle. DO NOT trim your ends with scissors without darning them in first as all your hard work will unravel. If this sounds complicated, don't worry, I'll have a post on how to do this later on.
                     <br/>
                     <br/>
                     Want to download a printable copy of this pattern? Click
                     <a href="http://www.ravelry.com/dls/tinselmint/183783?filename=" target="_blank">
                      here
                     </a>
                     .
                     <br/>
                     <br/>
                     Style and wear how you like! I'll post pics soon on the many ways to wear this lovely seed stitch infinity scarf (you can find that post
                     <a href="http://www.tinselmint.com/2013/07/how-to-wear-infinity-scarf.html" target="_blank">
                      here
                     </a>
                     ). Let me know you've cast on by leaving a comment below or tweeting me
                     <a href="https://twitter.com/tinselmint">
                      @tinselmint
                     </a>
                     . I can't wait to see your progress, happy knitting.
                     <br/>
                     <br/>
                     <div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;">
                      <a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-MJmQ7qgE6K8/UVCEQs9q74I/AAAAAAAAALA/mW1TD38Mx6s/s1600/SIGNATURE+2.png" imageanchor="1" style="">
                       <img border="0" src="https://lh3.ggpht.com/-MJmQ7qgE6K8/UVCEQs9q74I/AAAAAAAAALA/mW1TD38Mx6s/s1600/SIGNATURE+2.png"/>
                      </a>
                     </div>
                     <br/>
                     <div style="clear: both;">
                     </div>
                    </div>

      var cursor = null;
      if (items &amp;&amp; items.length &gt; 0) {
        cursor = parseInt(items[items.length - 1].timestamp) + 1;
      }

      var bodyFromEntry = function(entry) {
        if (entry.gd$extendedProperty) {
          for (var k in entry.gd$extendedProperty) {
            if (entry.gd$extendedProperty[k].name == 'blogger.contentRemoved') {
              return '&lt;span class="deleted-comment"&gt;' + entry.content.$t + '&lt;/span&gt;';
            }
          }
        }
        return entry.content.$t;
      }

      var parse = function(data) {
        cursor = null;
        var comments = [];
        if (data &amp;&amp; data.feed &amp;&amp; data.feed.entry) {
          for (var i = 0, entry; entry = data.feed.entry[i]; i++) {
            var comment = {};
            // comment ID, parsed out of the original id format
            var id = /blog-(\d+).post-(\d+)/.exec(entry.id.$t);
            comment.id = id ? id[2] : null;
            comment.body = bodyFromEntry(entry);
            comment.timestamp = Date.parse(entry.published.$t) + '';
            if (entry.author &amp;&amp; entry.author.constructor === Array) {
              var auth = entry.author[0];
              if (auth) {
                comment.author = {
                  name: (auth.name ? auth.name.$t : undefined),
                  profileUrl: (auth.uri ? auth.uri.$t : undefined),
                  avatarUrl: (auth.gd$image ? auth.gd$image.src : undefined)
                };
              }
            }
            if (entry.link) {
              if (entry.link[2]) {
                comment.link = comment.permalink = entry.link[2].href;
              }
              if (entry.link[3]) {
                var pid = /.*comments\/default\/(\d+)\?.*/.exec(entry.link[3].href);
                if (pid &amp;&amp; pid[1]) {
                  comment.parentId = pid[1];
                }
              }
            }
            comment.deleteclass = 'item-control blog-admin';
            if (entry.gd$extendedProperty) {
              for (var k in entry.gd$extendedProperty) {
                if (entry.gd$extendedProperty[k].name == 'blogger.itemClass') {
                  comment.deleteclass += ' ' + entry.gd$extendedProperty[k].value;
                } else if (entry.gd$extendedProperty[k].name == 'blogger.displayTime') {
                  comment.displayTime = entry.gd$extendedProperty[k].value;
                }
              }
            }
            comments.push(comment);
          }
        }
        return comments;
      };

      var paginator = function(callback) {
        if (hasMore()) {
          var url = config.feed + '?alt=json&amp;v=2&amp;orderby=published&amp;reverse=false&amp;max-results=50';
          if (cursor) {
            url += '&amp;published-min=' + new Date(cursor).toISOString();
          }
          window.bloggercomments = function(data) {
            var parsed = parse(data);
            cursor = parsed.length &lt; 50 ? null
                : parseInt(parsed[parsed.length - 1].timestamp) + 1
            callback(parsed);
            window.bloggercomments = null;
          }
          url += '&amp;callback=bloggercomments';
          var script = document.createElement('script');
          script.type = 'text/javascript';
          script.src = url;
          document.getElementsByTagName('head')[0].appendChild(script);
        }
      };
      var hasMore = function() {
        return !!cursor;
      };
      var getMeta = function(key, comment) {
        if ('iswriter' == key) {
          var matches = !!comment.author
              &amp;&amp; comment.author.name == config.authorName
              &amp;&amp; comment.author.profileUrl == config.authorUrl;
          return matches ? 'true' : '';
        } else if ('deletelink' == key) {
          return config.baseUri + '/delete-comment.g?blogID='
               + config.blogId + '&amp;postID=' + comment.id;
        } else if ('deleteclass' == key) {
          return comment.deleteclass;
        }
        return '';
      };

      var replybox = null;
      var replyUrlParts = null;
      var replyParent = undefined;

      var onReply = function(commentId, domId) {
        if (replybox == null) {
          // lazily cache replybox, and adjust to suit this style:
          replybox = document.getElementById('comment-editor');
          if (replybox != null) {
            replybox.height = '250px';
            replybox.style.display = 'block';
            replyUrlParts = replybox.src.split('#');
          }
        }
        if (replybox &amp;&amp; (commentId !== replyParent)) {
          document.getElementById(domId).insertBefore(replybox, null);
          replybox.src = replyUrlParts[0]
              + (commentId ? '&amp;parentID=' + commentId : '')
              + '#' + replyUrlParts[1];
          replyParent = commentId;
        }
      };

      var hash = (window.location.hash || '#').substring(1);
      var startThread, targetComment;
      if (/^comment-form_/.test(hash)) {
        startThread = hash.substring('comment-form_'.length);
      } else if (/^c[0-9]+$/.test(hash)) {
        targetComment = hash.substring(1);
      }

      // Configure commenting API:
      var configJso = {
        'maxDepth': config.maxThreadDepth
      };
      var provider = {
        'id': config.postId,
        'data': items,

      };

          var holder = document.getElementById('comment-holder');
          window.goog.comments.render(holder, provider);
        }
      };

      // render now, or queue to render when library loads:

                                  <p class="comment-content" id="bc_0_2MC">
                                   If you are using the same yarn as I used, I think a size 13US needle will make the scarf very loose and lacy. I would suggest sizing down your needles. But, if you are ok with loose and lacy I would suggest casting on less stitches...try 23 stitches and see how that looks.

                                   Happy Knitting

                                  <p class="comment-content" id="bc_0_9MC">
                                   You can most definitely change the stitch pattern to a basic knit stitch. It will possibly change the width of the scarf, but if you are ok with that just go for it!
                                   <br/>
                                   If you are up for it, you could try knitting a test swatch in the knit stitch to see what you gauge is and compare it to the gauge in the pattern about.
                                   <br/>
                                   Let me know how it goes and happy knitting.
                                  </p>

                                <li class="comment" id="bc_0_11B" kind="b">
                                 <div class="avatar-image-container">
                                  <img src="http://img2.blogblog.com/img/b36-rounded.png"/>
                                 </div>
                                 <div class="comment-block" id="c5020626779960364977">
                                  <div class="comment-header" id="bc_0_11M" kind="m">
                                   <cite class="user blog-author">
                                    <a href="https://www.blogger.com/profile/05212994918238792015" rel="nofollow">
                                     Michelle Czyzo
                                    </a>
                                   </cite>
                                   <span class="icon user blog-author">
                                   </span>
                                   <span class="datetime secondary-text">
                                    <a href="http://www.tinselmint.com/2013/05/free-infinity-scarf-pattern-for.html?showComment=1384472262490&amp;m=1#c5020626779960364977" rel="nofollow">
                                     November 14, 2013 at 6:37 PM
                                    </a>
                                   </span>
                                  </div>
                                  <p class="comment-content" id="bc_0_11MC">
                                   It is possible, although I haven't tried it myself. Here is what I have calculated:
                                   <br/>
                                   <br/>
                                   My scarf measures 10.5 inches across and is 33 stitches...that's roughly 3 stitches per inch. If we use this ratio to convert the expected finished length of the scarf (61 inches) to stitches so we know how many to cast on. 61 multiplied by 3 is 183 stitches.
                                   <br/>
                                   <br/>
                                   I would try casting on 183 stitches unto circular needles then k1p1 continuously and that should work. Also, knit until the width of the scarf measures 10.5 inches and then cast off.
                                   <br/>
                                   <br/>
                                   Let me know how this turns out for you ;)
                                  </p>

                                  <p class="comment-content" id="bc_0_21MC">
                                   Hi Debbie
                                   <br/>
                                   <br/>
                                   I'm sure if you ask the staff to help you find a bulky weight yarn / number 5 they will be able to help you find something suitable. Billow, the yarn I used, also has a thick and thin texture so you would even be ok with choosing from a medium weight yarn / number 4. Billow is cotton but use whatever you prefer and like the feel of (ie: wool, alpaca, bamboo, acrylic). Let me know what you choose and happy knitting!
                                  </p>

                            <p class="comment-content" id="bc_0_28MC">
                             I don't think it really matters how "wide" your scarf is - I started making one myself this week and went with personal preference.  As far as the needle size - you can use size 13 US needs, but depending on the yarn, your scarf may have a more "relaxed" look to it.
                             <br/>
                             <br/>
                             Remember, there is no wrong way, work with what you have to get the look you'd like.  I hope you enjoy your scarf!
                            </p>

                                   I used Red Heart wool and it turned out beautiful!

                                  <p class="comment-content" id="bc_0_32MC">
                                   You can substitute with any yarn that has the same weight. Billow is a "bulky" weight yarn or a "five". You can find this info on the yarn label.
                                   <br/>
                                   <br/>
                                   Read my SIZE MATTERS post and take a look at the YARN STANDARDS CHART by following the link below:
                                   <br/>
                                   <br/>
                                   http://www.tinselmint.com/2013/04/size-matters-yarn-and-knitting-needles.html
                                   <br/>
                                   <br/>
                                   <br/>
                                  </p>

                            <p class="comment-content" id="bc_0_41MC">
                             You can substitute with any yarn that has the same weight. Billow is a "bulky" weight yarn or a "five". You can find this info on the yarn label.
                             <br/>
                             <br/>
                             Read my SIZE MATTERS post and take a look at the YARN STANDARDS CHART by following the link below:
                             <br/>
                             <br/>
                             http://www.tinselmint.com/2013/04/size-matters-yarn-and-knitting-needles.html
                            </p>

                                  <p class="comment-content" id="bc_0_53MC">
                                   Read my SIZE MATTERS post and take a look at the YARN STANDARDS CHART by following the link below (I've also linked to it in the post above):
                                   <br/>
                                   <br/>
                                   http://www.tinselmint.com/2013/04/size-matters-yarn-and-knitting-needles.html
                                   <br/>
                                   <br/>
                                   You should still be able to use the size 11 US needle but you can also use larger. Keep in mind using a larger yarn and/or larger needles will not give you the same results as the scarf I've knit. Your scarf will be chunkier and the size will be larger overall.
                                   <br/>
                                  </p>

                                  <p class="comment-content" id="bc_0_67MC">
                                   It took me somewhere around 8 days. Keep in mind I was also writing the pattern while knitting and doing instructional pictures and posts. Also everyone is different and it may take one person a day or another a month. Go at your own pace. This is a great pattern for beginners to practice as it involves repetition of the same stitch pattern.
                                  </p>

                             Hi
                             <br/>
                             Thank you for this pattern. I already have worsted weight 4 yard (2 balls of 140 grams) which comes with a recommendation to use size US 8 needles. Can I still use this pattern as long as the width is approximately 10-11 inches? How many stitches should I cast on?
                             <br/>
                             <br/>
                             Thanks for your help!

                                  <div class="comment-header" id="bc_0_69M" kind="m">
                                   <cite class="user blog-author">
                                    <a href="https://www.blogger.com/profile/05212994918238792015" rel="nofollow">

                                    <a href="http://www.tinselmint.com/2013/05/free-infinity-scarf-pattern-for.html?showComment=1387648976261&amp;m=1#c7168771778467893657" rel="nofollow">
                                     December 21, 2013 at 1:02 PM
                                    </a>
                                   </span>
                                  </div>
                                  <p class="comment-content" id="bc_0_69MC">
                                   Hi there :)
                                   <br/>
                                   <br/>
                                   A worsted weight yarn is a number 4 / medium weight yarn which is slightly smaller than a number 5 / bulky weight yarn that is recommended for this pattern...However, given that Billow (the yarn I used) has a thick and thin texture, I think you would be ok to use the worsted weight yarn. Try casting on the suggested number of stitches and knitting a few rows to see how the width turns out. If it is not wide enough (10.5 inches) than I would even suggest just going up a needle size to a US 9 as worsted weight yarn needle sizes are generally anywhere from a 7-9 US.
                                   <br/>
                                   I hope this helps and happy knitting!
                                   <br/>
                                  </p>

                                  <p class="comment-content" id="bc_0_83MC">
                                   Bulky is the type of yarn called for in the pattern. So, the answer to your question is no. And no you do not need to twist the scarf before stitching the ends together. The twisting happens as you wrap the scarf around your neck once its complete.
                                  </p>

                                  <p class="comment-content" id="bc_0_91MC">
                                   That is correct...it is a basic K1 P1 stitch. When you turn your work over your first stitch will be a knit stitch. The reason this is possible is a result of the number of stitches cast on. When working K1 P1 you should always cast on an uneven number of stitches (just like in my pattern) so that each row is knit the same. If there was an even number of stitches cast on you would, like you suggested, have to knit the reverse (P1 K1) on the second row.
                                   <br/>
                                   I hope this helps dissolve the confusion :)
                                  </p>
                                  <span class="comment-actions secondary-text" id="bc_0_91MN" kind="m">

                                  <p class="comment-content" id="bc_0_93MC">
                                   Hi Erma,
                                   <br/>
                                   I hope so :) That is correct, although the twist is optional (some patterns have it some don't). For the purpose of teaching beginners how to knit I thought straight needles would be best. However, if you are familiar with using circulars needles, they are a great choice for creating a "no seam" infinity scarf. If you are looking to try it, I make suggestions for this pattern on circulars in the comments above.
                                  </p>

                                   <cite class="user blog-author">

                                    <a href="http://www.tinselmint.com/2013/05/free-infinity-scarf-pattern-for.html?showComment=1390437318822&amp;m=1#c5366321784764320095" rel="nofollow">
                                     January 22, 2014 at 7:35 PM

                                  </div>
                                  <p class="comment-content" id="bc_0_95MC">
                                   How old is your granddaughter? By casting on less stitches you change the width of the scarf when perhaps it may be better to change the length. I would suggest keeping the original cast on and then shortening the length / number of rows knit. Ideally when the scarf is hanging in a single loop around her neck it should hit a couple of inches above her belly button. This will allow enough room for doubling the scarf around her neck. Try knitting until you've reached 50 inches and then measure it against her.
                                   <br/>
                                   Also if you do decide to cast on less stitches and make the scarf narrower, you should always cast on an uneven number of stitches.
                                   <br/>
                                   Hope this helps and happy knitting.
                                  </p>
                                  <span class="comment-actions secondary-text" id="bc_0_95MN" kind="m">

                                 </div>
                                 <div class="comment-block" id="c1067752655733138285">
                                  <div class="comment-header" id="bc_0_97M" kind="m">
                                   <cite class="user blog-author">
                                    <a href="https://www.blogger.com/profile/05212994918238792015" rel="nofollow">
                                     Michelle Czyzo
                                    </a>
                                   </cite>
                                   <span class="icon user blog-author">
                                   </span>
                                   <span class="datetime secondary-text">
                                    <a href="http://www.tinselmint.com/2013/05/free-infinity-scarf-pattern-for.html?showComment=1392161595233&amp;m=1#c1067752655733138285" rel="nofollow">
                                     February 11, 2014 at 6:33 PM
                                    </a>
                                   </span>
                                  </div>
                                  <p class="comment-content" id="bc_0_97MC">
                                   At the moment, no I do not...Maybe sometime in the future.
                                   <br/>
                                   <br/>
                                   If you are inclined to give it a go with out a pattern I would suggest casting on enough stitches to have it go around her head on circular needles. Then knit several rounds until it is nice and long (the length makes it slouchy). Once it's long enough, draw the yarn through the remaining stitches to make a gathered top (no need to worry about tapering with a slouchy hat).
                                   <br/>
                                   <br/>
                                   Happy knitting!
                                  </p>

                              <ol class="thread-chrome thread-expanded" id="bc_0_107TC">
                               <div>
                                <li class="comment" id="bc_0_107B" kind="b">
                                 <div class="avatar-image-container">
                                  <img src="http://img2.blogblog.com/img/b36-rounded.png"/>
                                 </div>
                                 <div class="comment-block" id="c1856658653256712324">
                                  <div class="comment-header" id="bc_0_107M" kind="m">
                                   <cite class="user blog-author">
                                    <a href="https://www.blogger.com/profile/05212994918238792015" rel="nofollow">
                                     Michelle Czyzo
                                    </a>
                                   </cite>
                                   <span class="icon user blog-author">
                                   </span>
                                   <span class="datetime secondary-text">
                                    <a href="http://www.tinselmint.com/2013/05/free-infinity-scarf-pattern-for.html?showComment=1402011051605&amp;m=1#c1856658653256712324" rel="nofollow">
                                     June 5, 2014 at 7:30 PM
                                    </a>
                                   </span>
                                  </div>
                                  <p class="comment-content" id="bc_0_107MC">
                                   Great question Erin!
                                   <br/>
                                   The easiest method is to add the new ball at the beginning of a row. So, when you see you are getting close to running out of yarn, finish that row if you can, or go back if you can't. At the beginning of your next row ignore the old yarn and start with the new ball. Be sure to leave a bit of a tail so that you have something to darn in later. Once you've knit a a row, you can tie the two tails at the beginning of the row together to keep them from unraveling while you work. I like to untie them later when I'm darning in my tails for a cleaner finish.
                                  </p>
                                  <span class="comment-actions secondary-text" id="bc_0_107MN" kind="m">
